    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="52024fd962904d75b37abb55b5b1d452">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f26c92b3-f18a-4312-88d0-e72a0f6fc523'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d9216109" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Antimony/porous biomass carbon nanocomposites have been prepared by a chemical reduction method and applied as anodes for sodium-ion batteries. The porous biomass carbon derived from a black fungus had a large Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 2233 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> in which antimony nanoparticles were uniformly distributed in the porous carbon. The as-prepared antimony/porous biomass carbon nanocomposites exhibited a high reversible sodium storage capacity of 567 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, extended cycling stability, and good rate capability.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201601398-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201601398-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much attention for large-scale electric energy storage and conversion owing to their low cost, the natural abundance of sodium resources, and similar reaction mechanisms to lithium-ion batteries.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0001_R_d9216101e344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></span> However, the radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (0.102 nm) is larger than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.076 nm), thus making the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction in the host materials very difficult.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0002_R_d9216101e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></span> Commercial graphite in particular is widely used for lithium-ion batteries but is not applicable for sodium-ion batteries. Compared with commercial graphite, hard carbon is more suitable for sodium storage owing to its natural nanopores on the surface, but its capacity is low (&lt;200 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0003_R_d9216101e357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></span> In addition, the potential of sodium insertion into carbon materials is very close to the sodium deposition potential, which easily leads to a safety problem when a slight polarization occurs.</p>
         
         <p>Many anode materials have been developed for sodium-ion batteries, including P,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0004_R_d9216101e362" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></span> Ge,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0005_R_d9216101e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></span> Sn,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0006_R_d9216101e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></span> and Sb.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0007_R_d9216101e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></span> Among the various Na−M alloying anode materials, Sb appears to be a promising candidate owing to its high theoretical capacity of 660 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (by forming Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy).<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0008_R_d9216101e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></span> However, bare Sb exhibits poor cycling stability owing to the large volume expansion (approximately 390 %) during the process of alloying/dealloying with Na. This causes pulverization of the Sb electrodes, thus resulting in poor electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0009_R_d9216101e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></span> To solve this problem, a simple method is to synthesize nanosized materials embedded in or coating conductive carbon matrices that can act as a buffer to accommodate the mechanical stress. Several nanosized Sb/carbon-based composites have been reported, including nano-Sb/C composites,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0010_R_d9216101e379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Commun.">10</a></span> Sb/C fibers,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0011_R_d9216101e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - ACS Nano">11</a></span> Sb−C-graphene fibrous composites,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0012_R_d9216101e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochim. Acta">12</a></span> pitaya-like Sb@C microspheres,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0013_R_d9216101e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Mater. Chem. A">13</a></span> spherical nano-Sb@C,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0014_R_d9216101e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Res.">14</a></span> and 3D Sb@RCN.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0015_R_d9216101e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></span> However, the complicated synthetic processes limit large-scale applications. Therefore, efforts are needed to simplify the synthetic procedures and further improve the electrochemical performances of Sb-based anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Relative to graphene and carbon nanotubes,<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0012_R_d9216101e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochim. Acta">12</a>, <a href="#asia201601398-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0016_R_d9216101e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Res. Bull.">16</a></span> porous biomass carbon costs less and is more easily prepared. Herein we synthesized Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites by a simple and low-cost method. Porous biomass carbon was prepared by using a black fungus as the precursor. The as-prepared porous carbon had a large Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area of 2233 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. When applied as an anode material in sodium-ion batteries, the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites exhibited a high reversible capacity of 567 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and good rate performance. Even at a current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the nanocomposites still delivered a capacity of 330 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> as anodes for sodium-ion batteries. The combination of high-capacity Sb nanoparticles with a porous biomass carbon matrix could be a promising strategy for developing high-performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201601398-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201601398-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The crystal phases of the porous carbon, bare Sb, and Sb/porous carbon (60 wt %) nanocomposites were identified by X-ray diffraction (XRD) as shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0001">1</a>. All the diffraction peaks could be satisfactorily indexed to the standard data of Sb (JCPDS card no. 35-0732) for bare Sb and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites. There is no sharp diffraction peak that corresponds to carbon in the XRD patterns of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites. Instead, a broad diffraction peak appeared in the XRD pattern of the porous carbon, which confirmed that the porous biomass carbon was amorphous. The mass ratio of Sb to porous carbon in the Sb/porous carbon sample was determined by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA; see the Supporting Information, which shows the TG curve of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites). The mass loading of Sb in the sample was determined to be 64.15 % and was consistent with the mass fraction to Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites (nominally 60 wt %) in the experiment. The weight loss of the sample at low temperature originated from the moisture in the sample. When the temperature increased to 470 °C, the weight began to decline as a result of the oxidation of carbon. After 660 °C, the weight was stable as the temperature increased; all the Sb particles reacted with O<sub>2</sub> (2 Sb+2 O<sub>2</sub>→Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) and porous biomass carbon was burned out to form CO<sub>2</sub> (C+O<sub>2</sub>→CO<sub>2</sub>). The mass fraction of Sb particles in the Sb/porous carbon composites was calculated according to the equations above. The XRD of the burnt product is shown in the Supporting Information. All the diffraction peaks can be indexed to the standard diffraction of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS card no. 17-0620).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cf4e31b1-762a-4279-8a26-13dfe9a9ce89/asia201601398-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cf4e31b1-762a-4279-8a26-13dfe9a9ce89/asia201601398-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4c9dcafc-c1b4-4230-a24b-60412f7e2f82/asia201601398-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cf4e31b1-762a-4279-8a26-13dfe9a9ce89/asia201601398-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>XRD patterns of porous carbon, bare Sb, and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0002">2</a> shows the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the porous carbon and Sb/porous carbon (60 wt %) nanocomposites. Porous biomass carbon exhibits a morphology with numerous pores (Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0002">2</a>a), and the SEM image of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites is shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0002">2</a>b for comparison. From the SEM image, we can see that Sb nanoparticles are dispersed in the porous biomass carbon, and some particles are on the surface of the porous biomass carbon. This structure could effectively alleviate the volume expansion when forming Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy. The obtained Sb nanoparticles had an average size of 300 nm (see the Supporting Information). The Supporting Information also shows additional SEM images of Sb/porous carbon (50, 70 wt %) nanocomposites. By increasing the amount of Sb, more Sb nanoparticles were observed in the SEM image of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites, which is consistent with the mass loading of Sb.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea4fb3f3-1d33-4dc7-95e4-4a9155ac03f9/asia201601398-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea4fb3f3-1d33-4dc7-95e4-4a9155ac03f9/asia201601398-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b735a4a6-b264-4873-aabf-a0e7c62dadaf/asia201601398-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea4fb3f3-1d33-4dc7-95e4-4a9155ac03f9/asia201601398-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>SEM images of a) porous carbon and b) Sb/porous carbon (60 wt %) nanocomposites.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore-size distribution curves of the porous biomass carbon are shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0003">3</a>. As can be seen in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0003">3</a>a, the isotherms show a steep increase at relatively high pressure, which implies that the porous biomass carbon contains a large amount of mesopores. Accordingly, porous biomass carbon has a large BET surface area of about 2233 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0003">3</a>b shows the pore-size distribution with an average pore size of 2.23 nm.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23cf84fd-238a-4302-8910-14e02b82a61d/asia201601398-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/23cf84fd-238a-4302-8910-14e02b82a61d/asia201601398-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8e8ea980-d0ce-4259-b207-5f4b5e5636bf/asia201601398-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23cf84fd-238a-4302-8910-14e02b82a61d/asia201601398-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The BET result for the porous biomass carbon a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm and b) pore-size distribution.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0004">4</a> shows the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images of the porous carbon and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites. The TEM analysis (Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0004">4</a>a) further confirmed the porous structure of the as-prepared carbon. Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0004">4</a>b clearly indicates that Sb nanoparticles were uniformly distributed in the porous biomass carbon matrix. The average size of the Sb nanoparticles was around 250 nm. The corresponding HRTEM image (Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0004">4</a>c) of the Sb in the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites displays good crystallinity and clear lattice fringes. The interplanar distance was determined to be 0.31 nm, which corresponds to the <i>d</i> spacing of the (012) plane. The selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern in the inset proves that the Sb nanoparticles are polycrystalline.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f4a3f819-2e20-430e-ad1d-8ee51a8c0ee9/asia201601398-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f4a3f819-2e20-430e-ad1d-8ee51a8c0ee9/asia201601398-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dff38e92-4136-444a-86c9-202dfaca69b2/asia201601398-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f4a3f819-2e20-430e-ad1d-8ee51a8c0ee9/asia201601398-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>TEM images of a) porous carbon and b) Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites. c) HRTEM and SAED pattern of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The electrochemical properties of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites were characterized by cyclic voltammograms. Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0005">5</a>a presents the CV curves of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a potential window between 0.01 and 2.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. During the first cathodic scan, a reduction peak was observed at 0.32 V, which corresponds to the formation of solid–electrolyte interface (SEI) layers<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0008a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0008a_R_d9216101e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8a - Energy Environ. Sci.">8a</a></span> and the Na<sup>+</sup> reaction with Sb to form Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0009b" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0009b_R_d9216101e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - J. Mater. Chem. A">9b</a>, <a href="#asia201601398-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0012_R_d9216101e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Electrochim. Acta">12</a>, <a href="#asia201601398-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0014_R_d9216101e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Res.">14</a>, <a href="#asia201601398-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0017_R_d9216101e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Electrochim. Acta">17</a></span> As a comparison, the Supporting Information shows the CV curves of the bare porous biomass carbon electrode. It can be seen that there is a cathodic peak at 0.32 V<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0010_R_d9216101e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Commun.">10</a></span> in the first scan and a reduction peak around 0.34 Vin the second scan. In the anodic scans, the peak at 0.88 V corresponds to the phase transition from the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloy to Sb. In the two subsequent scans, the CV curves show two reduction peaks at 0.54 and 0.687 V, which can be assigned to the Na alloying reaction with Sb to form new Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0009b" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0009b_R_d9216101e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9b - J. Mater. Chem. A">9b</a></span> and the insertion reaction of Na into the porous biomass carbon matrix, respectively.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0018_R_d9216101e592" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></span> The difference is probably due to the formation of SEI layers and the activation process in the first and subsequent cycles. The CV curves of Sb and Sb/porous carbon (50, 70 wt %) nanocomposites are shown in the Supporting Information.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8949c7a1-3c26-47be-838c-77b3d1390abf/asia201601398-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8949c7a1-3c26-47be-838c-77b3d1390abf/asia201601398-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7679b404-c591-44bc-b0bd-750ed3cfbdd1/asia201601398-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8949c7a1-3c26-47be-838c-77b3d1390abf/asia201601398-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Cyclic voltammograms of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in potential window between 0.01 and 2.0 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0005">5</a>b shows the charge/discharge profiles of the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites between 0.01 and 2.0 V at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the first, second, and third cycles. During the first cycle, there is a plateau between 0.55 and 0.28 V in the initial discharge, which represents the formation of SEI layers and Na alloying with Sb to form Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. A plateau between 0.76 and 0.85 V in the first charge is related to the dealloying process, which is consistent with the first CV curve. The second discharge curve exhibits a sloping region between 0.70 and 0.32 V, which corresponds to the alloying reaction to form Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. These observations are highly consistent with the CV curves as shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0005">5</a>a. In the first sodiation process, the electrode delivered a discharge capacity of 902 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 534 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency of 62.82 %. The Supporting Information shows the charge/discharge curves of the bare Sb and other Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites (50, 70 wt %) electrodes, which have higher irreversible capacities. As also shown in the Supporting Information, the Coulombic efficiencies gradually increased and maintained a stable range around 99 % in subsequent cycles.</p>
         
         <p>The cyclability of the electrode was further tested by means of long-term cycling. Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0006">6</a>a shows the cycling performance of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. From the second cycle, we can see that the composites maintained a high capacity retention after 250 cycles. For comparison, Sb/porous carbon (50, 70 wt %) and bare porous biomass carbon electrodes were also tested over 100 cycles (see the Supporting Information). They exhibited good cycling stabilities over 100 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites (60 wt %) exhibited the highest capacity among all three composites. However, the bare Sb electrode showed very poor cycling performance. The electrode exhibited a high initial reversible capacity of 452 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with a Coulombic efficiency of 60.5 %. However, the capacity faded quickly owing to the large volume expansion in the discharge/charge process. The capacity degraded to 80 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 80 cycles. When Sb nanoparticles were added in biomass carbon, the capacity was improved with good stabilities over 250 cycles. The capacity of Sb/porous carbon electrodes was improved owing to the conductive porous biomass carbon matrix, which has a large BET surface area that can act as a buffer to adjust the mechanical stress and large volume expansion during the sodiation/desodiation process and alleviate particle aggregation as well as provide electronic conduction.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b5a35284-ad37-4d21-afef-563049a8b3b8/asia201601398-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b5a35284-ad37-4d21-afef-563049a8b3b8/asia201601398-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fd9c76d1-319b-448c-8929-410df957af5c/asia201601398-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b5a35284-ad37-4d21-afef-563049a8b3b8/asia201601398-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>a) Cycling performance of bare Sb and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and b) rate performance.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The rate performance of the as-prepared Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites was tested at various current densities from 100 to 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites electrode exhibited a reversible capacity of 572, 554, 510, 481, 453, and 330 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 100, 200, 300, 400, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These rate performances are shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0006">6</a>b. Even at a current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity was 330 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and the capacity was almost recovered when the current density returned to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which indicated good rate performance for Na-ion insertion/extraction. Sb nanoparticles dispersed in the porous biomass carbon seemed to alleviate the aggregation of Sb nanoparticles and improve electrolyte infiltration, thus leading to better rate performance. Compared to electrode materials, the rate performance of the Sb/porous carbon (60 wt %) nanocomposites was better than Sb/porous carbon (50 wt %) and nanocomposites (70 wt %). From the comparison, we can conclude that the precise mass fraction of Sb nanoparticles is very important to achieve both high capacity and good cycle life.</p>
         
         <p>We also performed AC impedance spectroscopy measurements to compare the electronic conductivity and charge-transfer dynamics of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites and bare Sb electrodes after 100 cycles, as shown in Figure <a href="#asia201601398-fig-0007">7</a>. The two profiles show similar features: two depressed semicircles and straight lines.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="asia201601398-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/991e6107-d968-4094-91bf-8b5a37ef6c48/asia201601398-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/991e6107-d968-4094-91bf-8b5a37ef6c48/asia201601398-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c5254e5b-7e34-4ccb-9fa9-502a7b0fe41b/asia201601398-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/991e6107-d968-4094-91bf-8b5a37ef6c48/asia201601398-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=asia201601398-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fasia.201601398" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>AC impedance spectroscopy of bare Sb and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In general, the semicircles in the high-frequency region are associated with the SEI resistances (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and the charge-transfer impedance charge (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) on the electrode/electrolyte interface, whereas the straight lines in the low-frequency region correlate to the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) associated with the diffusion of sodium ions in the bulk of the electrode material.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0002a" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0002a_R_d9216101e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2a - J. Electrochem. Soc.">2a</a>, <a href="#asia201601398-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0019_R_d9216101e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></span> The semicircle size for the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites electrode is much smaller than the bare Sb electrode. This confirmed that the charge-transfer resistance of Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites was lower than that of the bare Sb electrodes. Porous biomass carbon can greatly enhance the electron transfer during electrochemical reactions and lead to better cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>The cycling performance of alloy anode materials fades quickly during alloying/dealloying processes owing to the large volume expansion of electrodes. The volume expansion usually results in particle aggregation and loss of contact between active materials and current collector/conductive additives.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0020_R_d9216101e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20">20</a></span> Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites can solve these problems because biomass carbon with large pores can serve as the conductive matrix to alleviate the large volume expansion and the aggregation of Sb particles during alloying/dealloying processes. The stable cycling performance of the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites electrode can be attributed to its capability to maintain the contact between Sb nanoparticles and porous biomass carbon conductive networks. The capacity was greatly improved relative to the bare Sb electrode.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201601398-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201601398-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we synthesized the porous biomass carbon and Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites using a low-cost and simple chemical reduction method. When applied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries, the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites exhibited high reversible capacity of 567 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a good rate performance in SIBs. The Sb/porous biomass carbon nanocomposites also demonstrated excellent cycling performance. This research could open a new avenue for developing high-performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries through combining high-capacity sodium alloying elements and low-cost biomass carbon.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="asia201601398-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201601398-sec-0004-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia201601398-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia201601398-sec-0005-title"> Chemicals</h3>
            
            <p>SbCl<sub>3</sub> (AR, 99 %), NaBH<sub>4</sub> (98 %), and KOH (AR) were purchased from Aladdin Reagent Co, Ltd, Shanghai, PR. China. Ethanol (AR, 99 %) and the black fungus were purchased from supermarket. All chemicals were used without purification.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia201601398-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia201601398-sec-0006-title"> Synthesis of Porous Biomass Carbon</h3>
            
            <p>The black fungus (3 g) was soaked in deionized water to wash away impurities. The precursors were transferred to a 100 mL autoclave reactor with deionized water (60 mL) and kept at 180 °C for 6 h. After washing with deionized water and ethanol, the product was obtained by drying in a vacuum oven at 60 °C overnight. The product was dissolved in deionized water with KOH (in a weight ratio of 1:3), stirred for 12 h, then dried under vacuum at 80 °C for 12 h. After this, the materials were heated at 800 °C for 1 h in an N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere, then naturally cooled to room temperature. The product was subsequently washed with 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> HCl and finally washed with deionized water to keep the pH at 7.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia201601398-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia201601398-sec-0007-title"> Synthesis of Sb/Porous Carbon Nanocomposites</h3>
            
            <p>The Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites (the weight ratio of Sb in the composites is 60 wt %) were prepared by a chemical reduction method using NaBH<sub>4</sub> as the reducing agent. In a typical experiment, porous biomass carbon (100 mg) from black fungus was dispersed in ethanol (50 mL), and was sonicated for 1 h. Then NaBH<sub>4</sub> (279.6 mg) was added into the above solution. Subsequently, SbCl<sub>3</sub> (562.08 mg) was added with continuous stirring for another 2 h at room temperature. After washing with deionized water and ethanol to remove the excess amount of Cl<sup>−</sup> ions as well as other ions, the final product was obtained by drying in a vacuum oven at 60 °C overnight. For comparison, bare Sb nanoparticles were also synthesized by means of the same procedure without adding porous biomass carbon. Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites with Sb of 50 and 70 wt % were also synthesized by the same procedure.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia201601398-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia201601398-sec-0008-title"> Materials Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal phases of the Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites were characterized by means of X-ray diffraction (XRD) with a Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu<sub>Kα</sub> radiation. The morphological observations were performed by using a scanning electron microscope (SU8010), transmission electron microscope (Hitachi H-7500), and high-resolution transmission electron microscope (FEI F20). TGA measurements were conducted with a TG-DSC instrument (NETZSCHSTA409PC) in a temperature range from 20 to 800 °C at 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup> heating rates in air.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="asia201601398-sec-0009">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="asia201601398-sec-0009-title"> Electrochemical Testing</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical properties of the obtained Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites for sodium-ion batteries were examined by means of cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge tests. The electrochemical tests were carried out using CR2032 coin cells. The electrode slurry was made by mixing 70 wt % Sb/porous carbon nanocomposites, 20 wt % acetylene black, and 10 wt % carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder in deionized water with grinding for 40 min to form a homogeneous slurry. The resultant slurry was then coated on a copper foil and dried in a vacuum oven at 110 °C for 12 h. The electrolyte was 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a mixture of propylene carbonate (PC) and ethylene carbonate (EC) electrolyte (volume ratio 1:1) in which 5 % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was added as additive.<span><a href="#asia201601398-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="asia201601398-bib-0021_R_d9216101e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></span> CR2032 coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox by using the as-prepared electrodes as working electrodes and a glass fiber filter (Whatman GF/D) as separator. The mass loading of the active material was about 0.8–1.1 mg (including the weight of biomass carbon). Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed by using a Land battery tester with the cutoff voltages set between 0.01 and 2.0 V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. AC impedance spectroscopy was carried out with a 5 mV amplitude in the frequency range between 0.01 Hz and 100 KHz using a CHI660E electrochemical workstation.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="asia201601398-sec-0010-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
